Text: Quantum Machine Learning (QML) models are aimed at learning from data encoded in quantum, Font: SFRM0900, Size: 8.966400146484375
Text: states. Recently, it has been shown that models with little to no inductive biases (i.e., with no, Font: SFRM0900, Size: 8.966400146484375
Text: assumptions about the problem embedded in the model) are likely to have trainability and gener-, Font: SFRM0900, Size: 8.966400146484375
Text: alization issues, especially for large problem sizes. As such, it is fundamental to develop schemes, Font: SFRM0900, Size: 8.966400146484375
Text: that encode as much information as available about the problem at hand. In this work we present, Font: SFRM0900, Size: 8.966400146484375
Text: a simple, yet powerful, framework where the underlying invariances in the data are used to build, Font: SFRM0900, Size: 8.966400146484375
Text: QML models that, by construction, respect those symmetries. These so-called group-invariant mod-, Font: SFRM0900, Size: 8.966400146484375
Text: els produce outputs that remain invariant under the action of any element of the symmetry group, Font: SFRM0900, Size: 8.966400146484375
Text: G, Font: EUFM10, Size: 8.966400146484375
Text:  associated to the dataset. We present theoretical results underpinning the design of, Font: SFRM0900, Size: 8.966400146484375
Text:  G, Font: EUFM10, Size: 8.966400146484375
Text: -invariant, Font: SFRM0900, Size: 8.966400146484375
Text: models, and exemplify their application through several paradigmatic QML classiﬁcation tasks in-, Font: SFRM0900, Size: 8.966400146484375
Text: cluding cases when, Font: SFRM0900, Size: 8.966400146484375
Text:  G, Font: EUFM10, Size: 8.966400146484375
Text:  is a continuous Lie group and also when it is a discrete symmetry group., Font: SFRM0900, Size: 8.966400146484375
Text: Notably, our framework allows us to recover, in an elegant way, several well known algorithms for, Font: SFRM0900, Size: 8.966400146484375
Text: the literature, as well as to discover new ones. Taken together, we expect that our results will help, Font: SFRM0900, Size: 8.966400146484375
Text: pave the way towards a more geometric and group-theoretic approach to QML model design., Font: SFRM0900, Size: 8.966400146484375

Text: Classiﬁcation accuracy, Font: SFBX0900, Size: 8.966400146484375
Text: Let us deﬁne some terminology that will allow us to, Font: SFRM1000, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: assess the accuracy of a model’s classiﬁcation. First, we, Font: SFRM1000, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: remark that we do not consider precision issues when dis-, Font: SFRM1000, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: cussing classiﬁcation accuracy. Recall that the model’s pre-, Font: SFRM1000, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: dictions in Eq. (, Font: SFRM1000, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: 2, Font: SFRM1000, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: ) are expectation values, which in practice, Font: SFRM1000, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: need to be estimated via measurements on a quantum com-, Font: SFRM1000, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: puter. Hence, given a ﬁnite number of shots (measurement, Font: SFRM1000, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: repetitions), these can only be resolved up to some addi-, Font: SFRM1000, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: tive errors. However, for the sake of simplicity, we here, Font: SFRM1000, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: assume the limit of zero shot noise (i.e., inﬁnite precision),, Font: SFRM1000, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: and we will challenge this assumption when appropriate, Font: SFRM1000, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: in the results section. With this remark in hand, consider, Font: SFRM1000, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: the following deﬁnitions of diﬀerent degrees of classiﬁcation, Font: SFRM1000, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: accuracy., Font: SFRM1000, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: Deﬁnition 4, Font: SFBX1000, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text:  (Classiﬁcation Accuracy), Font: SFRM1000, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: ., Font: SFBX1000, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text:  i) We say that a, Font: SFTI1000, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: model provides no information that can classify the data, Font: SFTI1000, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: if its outputs are always the same irrespective of the label, Font: SFTI1000, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: associated to the input quantum state. ii) We say that a, Font: SFTI1000, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: model performs noisy classiﬁcation if its outputs are the, Font: SFTI1000, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: same for some, but not all, data in diﬀerent classes. iii) We, Font: SFTI1000, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: say that a model perfectly classiﬁes the data if its outputs, Font: SFTI1000, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: are never the same for data in diﬀerent classes., Font: SFTI1000, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: We note that in some cases a model can, at best, only, Font: SFRM1000, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: perform noisy classiﬁcation as its accuracy will be funda-, Font: SFRM1000, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: mentally limited by the distinguishability of the quantum, Font: SFRM1000, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: states in the dataset. Note that this is typically not an is-, Font: SFRM1000, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: sue for classical datasets, although the issue does arise for, Font: SFRM1000, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: noisy classical data. In some cases, for example as in the, Font: SFRM1000, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: time-reversal dataset that we consider below, the quantum, Font: SFRM1000, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: data states associated with diﬀerent output labels are non-, Font: SFRM1000, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: orthogonal. In this case, perfect classiﬁcation cannot be, Font: SFRM1000, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: achieved, regardless of the form of the model., Font: SFRM1000, Size: 9.962599754333496
